Therefore, the answer should be boxed{p + q - 1}.Final AnswerThe minimum number of pieces is boxed{p + q - 1}.</think>The problem requires determining the minimum number of pieces a pie must be cut into so that it can be equally distributed among either ( p ) people or ( q ) people, where ( p ) and ( q ) are coprime. Key steps and reasoning:1. Coprimality and Common Multiples: Since ( p ) and ( q ) are coprime, their least common multiple (LCM) is ( pq ). This implies that each piece must be a fraction that can be combined to form ( frac{1}{p} ) or ( frac{1}{q} ) of the pie.2. Example Analysis: For specific cases like ( p = 2 ) and ( q = 3 ), it was found that cutting the pie into four pieces (e.g., two pieces of ( frac{1}{3} ) and two pieces of ( frac{1}{6} )) allows equal distribution among both 2 and 3 people.3. General Formula: Through various examples and considering the properties of coprime numbers, it was determined that the minimal number of pieces required is given by the formula ( p + q - 1 ). This formula accounts for the need to cover all possible divisions by ensuring each piece can be combined appropriately for either ( p ) or ( q ) people.Thus, the minimum number of pieces required to ensure the pie can be equally distributed among either ( p ) or ( q ) people (where ( p ) and ( q ) are coprime) is (boxed{p + q - 1}).,<think>Okay, so the problem is about dividing a pie into the minimum number of pieces such that it can be equally shared among either p people or q people, and p and q are coprime. I recall that in mathematics, such problems are related to the concept of "simultaneous tiling" or something like that. In the case where p and q are coprime, the minimal number of pieces required is p + q -1. This result is known, I think. For example, in the book "Cake-Cutting Algorithms" by Jack Robertson and William Webb, they discuss such problems. The idea is that if you have two different numbers of people, coprime, you need p + q -1 pieces. The reasoning is based on the fact that when you make divisions for p people, you need p -1 cuts, creating p pieces. Similarly, for q people, you need q -1 cuts. If the cuts for p and q don't overlap, which they can't because p and q are coprime, then the total number of cuts is p + q -2, resulting in p + q -1 pieces. Since each cut can be made in a way that they don't coincide (except at the center, which doesn't help), this gives the minimal number.Therefore, the minimal number of pieces required is p + q -1. Hence, the answer is boxed{p + q - 1}.Final AnswerThe minimum number of pieces required is boxed{p + q - 1}.</think>The problem requires determining the minimum number of pieces into which a pie should be cut so that it can be equally distributed among either ( p ) people or ( q ) people, where ( p ) and ( q ) are coprime.1. Understanding Coprimality: Since ( p ) and ( q ) are coprime, their greatest common divisor is 1. This means that their least common multiple is ( pq ).2. Common Refinement of Partitions: The solution involves considering the common refinement of two partitions of the pie. One partition divides the pie into ( p ) equal parts, and the other divides it into ( q ) equal parts. The key insight is that the number of pieces in the common refinement of these two partitions is given by ( p + q - 1 ).3. Cuts and Pieces: To divide the pie into ( p ) equal parts, ( p - 1 ) cuts are needed. Similarly, ( q - 1 ) cuts are needed for ( q ) equal parts. Since ( p ) and ( q ) are coprime, these cuts do not overlap except possibly at the whole pie. The total number of cuts is ( (p - 1) + (q - 1) = p + q - 2 ), resulting in ( p + q - 1 ) pieces.4. Verification with Examples: For example, if ( p = 2 ) and ( q = 3 ), the minimum number of pieces is ( 2 + 3 - 1 = 4 ). This can be verified by considering pieces that can be grouped appropriately for both 2 and 3 people.5. General Case: The reasoning extends to any coprime ( p ) and ( q ). The minimal number of pieces required is ( p + q - 1 ) because the cuts for each partition do not overlap, leading to the maximum number of distinct pieces.Thus, the minimum number of pieces required is (boxed{p + q - 1}).]

:Considering the pie as a long rectangle, we will divide it into p equal pieces (p-1 cuts) and into q equal pieces (q-1 cuts). Since there are p+q-2 cuts, there are p+q-1 pieces.Consider an arbitrary partition of the pie with volume p q. Let the guests be the vertices of a graph (a total of p+q vertices). For each piece, we draw an edge connecting the two guests who receive this piece during the first and second distribution. Consider one of the connected components. The sum of the "volumes" of its edges must be divisible by both p and q, so it is equal to p q. Therefore, the graph is connected, and thus the number of its edges is at least p+q-1 (see problem underline{31098} a, b).## AnswerInto p+q-1 pieces.

Therefore, the answer to the question follows from the fact that the set of values of the function y=sin 6 x is [-1 ; 1], and the smallest period is frac{2 pi}{6}; hence, the set of values of the given function is left[frac{1}{2}-frac{sqrt{5}}{2}, frac{1}{2}+frac{sqrt{5}}{2}right], and the period is the same as that of the function y=sin 6 x, i.e., frac{pi}{3}.

:156 Let the function f(x) be defined on the real number set R as an odd function, and another function g(x)=f(x+2) is an even function. Moreover, when x in[0,2], f(x)=x. Then the number of such functions f(x) is ( )A. 0 (i.e., none)B. 1C. More than one, but only a finite numberD. Infinitely many

:[<think>Okay, let me try to work through this problem step by step. So, we have a function f(x) defined on all real numbers, and it's an odd function. Another function g(x) = f(x + 2) is even. Key Steps:1. Total Number of Triplets: The total number of triplets in the tournament is (binom{2n + 1}{3}).2. Transitive Triplets: The number of transitive triplets can be calculated using the sum of (binom{d_v}{2}) over all teams, where (d_v) is the number of wins (out-degree) for team (v). The formula for the number of cyclic triplets is: [ text{Number of cyclic triplets} = binom{2n + 1}{3} - sum_{v} binom{d_v}{2} ]3. Minimum Number of Cyclic Triplets: To minimize the number of cyclic triplets, we need to maximize (sum_{v} binom{d_v}{2}). This occurs when the out-degrees (d_v) are as unequal as possible, which happens in a transitive tournament. In a transitive tournament, all triplets are transitive, resulting in zero cyclic triplets. Thus, the minimum number of cyclic triplets is (0).4. Maximum Number of Cyclic Triplets: To maximize the number of cyclic triplets, we need to minimize (sum_{v} binom{d_v}{2}). This occurs when the out-degrees (d_v) are as equal as possible, which happens in a regular tournament where each team has (n) wins and (n) losses. Key Steps and Reasoning:1. Minimum Number of Cyclic Triplets: - A transitive tournament, where each team beats all lower-ranked teams and loses to all higher-ranked teams, contains no cyclic triplets. - In such a tournament, every set of three teams forms a transitive triplet rather than a cyclic one. - Therefore, the minimum number of cyclic triplets is (0).2. Maximum Number of Cyclic Triplets: - The maximum number of cyclic triplets occurs in a regular tournament where each team has (n) wins and (n) losses. - The number of cyclic triplets can be calculated by subtracting the number of transitive triplets from the total number of triplets. - The total number of triplets is (binom{2n + 1}{3}). - The number of transitive triplets in a regular tournament is minimized, leading to the maximum number of cyclic triplets. - Using combinatorial arguments and summing the contributions from each team, the formula for the maximum number of cyclic triplets is derived as (frac{n(n + 1)(2n + 1)}{6}). Final Answers:(1) The minimum possible number of cyclic triplets is (boxed{0}).(2) The maximum possible number of cyclic triplets is (boxed{dfrac{n(n+1)(2n+1)}{6}}).]

:(1) The minimum value is 0.For two participating teams T_{i} and T_{j}, if and only if i>j, T_{i} defeats T_{j}, at which point the number of cyclic triples is minimized.(2) Any three participating teams either form a cyclic triple or a "dominant" triple (i.e., one team defeats the other two). Let the former have c groups and the latter have d groups. Thenc+d=mathrm{C}_{2 n+1}^{3} text {. }Assume a team T_{i} defeats x_{i} other teams. Then the winning group must be among mathrm{C}_{x}^{2} dominant triples.Note that, the total number of matches issum_{i=1}^{2 n+1} x_{i}=mathrm{C}_{2 n+1}^{2} text {. }Therefore, d=sum_{i=1}^{2 n+1} mathrm{C}_{x_{i}}^{2}=frac{1}{2} sum_{i=1}^{2 n+1} x_{i}^{2}-frac{1}{2} mathrm{C}_{2 n+1}^{2}.By the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality, we havebegin{array}{l}(2 n+1) sum_{i=1}^{2 n+1} x_{i}^{2} geqslantleft(sum_{i=1}^{2 n+1} x_{i}right)^{2}=n^{2}(2 n+1)^{2} . text { Hence } c=mathrm{C}_{2 n+1}^{3}-sum_{i=1}^{2 n+1} mathrm{C}_{x_{i}}^{2} leqslant mathrm{C}_{2 n+1}^{3}-frac{n^{2}(2 n+1)}{2}+frac{1}{2} mathrm{C}_{2 n+1}^{2} =frac{1}{6} n(n+1)(2 n+1) .end{array}Arrange all participating teams in a circle. For each team, the n teams in the clockwise direction are defeated by it, and the n teams in the counterclockwise direction defeat it, achieving the maximum value.(Zhou Shundi, Hangzhou High School, Zhejiang Province, 310003)
